<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome assembly of asparagus bean, Vigna unguiculata ssp. sesquipedialis"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Data 2019 6:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-07-17"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Asparagus bean (Vigna. unguiculata ssp. sesquipedialis), known for its very long and tender green pods, is an important vegetable crop broadly grown in the developing Asian countries. In this study, we reported a 632.8&#8201;Mb assembly (549.81&#8201;Mb non-N size) of asparagus bean based on the whole genome shotgun sequencing strategy. We also generated a linkage map for asparagus bean, which helped anchor 94.42% of the scaffolds into 11 pseudo-chromosomes. A total of 42,609 protein-coding genes and 3,579 non-protein-coding genes were predicted from the assembly. Taken together, these genomic resources of asparagus bean will help develop a pan-genome of V. unguiculata and facilitate the investigation of economically valuable traits in this species, so that the cultivation of this plant would help combat the protein and energy malnutrition in the developing world.

data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Yang-Dong" data-author-popup="auth-Yang-Dong" data-corresp-id="c3">Yang Dong<svg             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Asparagus bean (<i>Vigna</i>. <i>unguiculata</i> ssp. <i>sesquipedialis</i>), known for its very long and tender green pods, is an important vegetable crop broadly grown in the developing Asian countries. In this study, we reported a 632.8âMb assembly (549.81âMb non-N size) of asparagus bean based on the whole genome shotgun sequencing strategy. We also generated a linkage map for asparagus bean, which helped anchor 94.42% of the scaffolds into 11 pseudo-chromosomes. A total of 42,609 protein-coding genes and 3,579 non-protein-coding genes were predicted from the assembly. Taken together, these genomic resources of asparagus bean will help develop a pan-genome of <i>V</i>. <i>unguiculata</i> and facilitate the investigation of economically valuable traits in this species, so that the cultivation of this plant would help combat the protein and energy malnutrition in the developing world.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Abs2" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content">

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background &amp; Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background &amp; Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Asparagus bean (<i>Vigna unguiculata</i> ssp. <i>sesquipedialis</i>, 2nâ=â2Ãâ=â22) is a warm-season and drought-tolerant subspecies of cowpea (<i>Vigna unguiculata</i>) with a wide cultivation area in East and Southeast Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Ehlers, J. D. &amp; Hall, A. E. Cowpea (Vigna unguiculata L. Walp.). Field Crops Research 53, 187â204 (1997)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d42154e783">1</a></sup>. This plant is also known as yardlong bean because of its characteristic pod that grows up to 50â100âcm in length<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Xu, P. et al. Development and polymorphism of Vigna unguiculata ssp. unguiculata microsatellite markers used for phylogenetic analysis in asparagus bean (Vigna unguiculata ssp. sesquipedialis (L.) Verdc.). Molecular Breeding 25, 675â684 (2010)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d42154e787">2</a></sup>. The long pod trait is believed to be the result of intensive local domestication after it was brought to Asia from sub-Saharan Africa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Fang, J., Chao, C.-C. T., Roberts, P. A. &amp; Ehlers, J. D. Genetic diversity of cowpea [Vigna unguiculata (L.) Walp.] in four West African and USA breeding programs as determined by AFLP analysis. Genetic Resources and Crop Evolution 54, 1197â1209 (2006)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d42154e792">3</a></sup>. Unlike the grain-type subspecies common cowpea (<i>Vigna</i>. <i>unguiculata</i> ssp. <i>unguiculata</i>, or black-eyed pea), asparagus bean is harvested while its pod is still tender, thereby providing a very good source of protein, minerals, vitamins, and dietary fiber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Jayathilake, C. et al. Cowpea: an overview on its nutritional facts and health benefits. J Sci Food Agric 98, 4793â4806 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42154e805">4</a></sup>. Due to the low requirement for cultivation management and its high nutritional value, asparagus bean is one of the top crops that help combat malnutrition and food insecurity in most developing countries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Xu, P. et al. Genomic regions, cellular components and gene regulatory basis underlying pod length variations in cowpea (V. unguiculata L. Walp). Plant Biotechnology Journal 15, 547â557 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d42154e809">5</a></sup>.</p><p>As the DNA sequencing technologies became more advanced and affordable for the past decade, previous research had mainly focused on delineating the genome of common cowpea (estimated genome size of 620 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Chen, X., Laudeman, T. W., Rushton, P. J., Spraggins, T. A. &amp; Timko, M. P. CGKB: an annotation knowledge base for cowpea (Vigna unguiculata L.) methylation filtered genomic genespace sequences. BMC Bioinformatics 8, 1â9 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d42154e816">6</a></sup>). The first study of cowpea genomics was reported in 2008, in which the gene-rich space of cowpea was sequenced and assembled into 52,149 assemblies (41,260 assemblies were annotated) and 70,679 singletons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Timko, M. P. et al. Sequencing and analysis of the gene-rich space of cowpea. BMC Genomics 9, 1â23 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d42154e820">7</a></sup>. Then the common cowpea (variety IT97K-499-35) genomic resources including a partial 323âMb whole-genome shotgun assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Munoz-Amatriain, M. et al. Genome resources for climate-resilient cowpea, an essential crop for food security. Plant J 89, 1042â1054 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d42154e824">8</a></sup>, a 497âMb bacterial artificial chromosome physical map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Munoz-Amatriain, M. et al. Genome resources for climate-resilient cowpea, an essential crop for food security. Plant J 89, 1042â1054 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d42154e828">8</a></sup>, and consensus genetic maps based on either 10 K<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Muchero, W. et al. A consensus genetic map of cowpea [Vigna unguiculata (L) Walp.] and synteny based on EST-derived SNPs. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 18159â18164 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d42154e832">9</a></sup> or 50âK single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) were available<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Munoz-Amatriain, M. et al. Genome resources for climate-resilient cowpea, an essential crop for food security. Plant J 89, 1042â1054 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d42154e837">8</a></sup>. A more recent research reported two survey genomes of common cowpea (varieties IT97K-499-35 and IT86D-1010) with substantially improved assembly sizes (568âMb and 609âMb, respectively)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Spriggs, A. et al. Assembled genomic and tissue-specific transcriptomic data resources for two genetically distinct lines of Cowpea (Vigna unguiculata (L.) Walp). Gates Open Research 2, 1â13 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d42154e841">10</a></sup>. In addition, a draft IT97K-499-35 variety reference genome was assembled by incorporating the single molecule real-time technology, yielding an assembly size of 519.4âMb into 722 scaffolds and 11 pseudo-chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Lonardi, S. et al. The genome of cowpea (Vigna unguiculata [L.] Walp.). Plant J 98, 767â782 (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d42154e845">11</a></sup>. Three genetic maps were derived from either simple sequence repeat markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Xu, P. et al. A SNP and SSR based genetic map of asparagus bean (Vigna. unguiculata ssp. sesquipedialis) and comparison with the broader species. PLoS One 6, e15952 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d42154e849">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Kongjaimun, A. et al. An SSR-based linkage map of yardlong bean (Vigna unguiculata (L.) Walp. subsp. unguiculata Sesquipedalis Group) and QTL analysis of pod length. Genome 55, 81â92 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d42154e852">13</a></sup> or restriction-site associated DNA sequencing for asparagus bean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Pan, L. et al. A High Density Genetic Map Derived from RAD Sequencing and Its Application in QTL Analysis of Yield-Related Traits in Vigna unguiculata. Front Plant Sci 8, 1â13 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d42154e856">14</a></sup>. Most of these genetic resources are focus on the grain-type cowpea, but there are many differences between the two types of cowpea, such as morphology, growing environments and parts for use<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Xu, P. et al. A SNP and SSR based genetic map of asparagus bean (Vigna. unguiculata ssp. sesquipedialis) and comparison with the broader species. PLoS One 6, e15952 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d42154e860">12</a></sup>.</p><p>In this study, we aimed to fill the knowledge gap with regard to the asparagus bean genome and provide new genetic resources for breeding cowpea and related legume species. A schematic workflow of the research is shown in Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Fig1">1</a>. In brief, a series of short-insert and large-insert Illumina libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 4000 platform, yielding a total of 222.9âGb clean data (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab1">1</a>). Since the genome size of asparagus bean was estimated to be about 590âMb using the <i>K</i>-mer distribution analysis (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab2">2</a>) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Fig2">2</a>), the clean data used for genome assembly represented about 340Ã coverage. The software SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. GigaScience 1, 1â6 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d42154e883">15</a></sup> was used to generate a draft contig assembly of 549.8âMb with a contig N50 size of 15.2âkb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab3">3</a>). After scaffolding and gap closing, the final asparagus genome was 632.8âMb (549.81âMb non-N size) in size with scaffold N50 size of 2.7âMb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab3">3</a>). We also obtained 536,824 high-confident SNPs from the whole-genome sequencing data of 97 asparagus bean F2 individuals and two parents from a well-controlled selfing population. These SNPs were used to construct a high-density genetic map for asparagus bean, in which 1,556 scaffolds were successfully anchored onto 11 pseudo-chromosomes (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab4">4</a>). Furthermore, the asparagus bean genome contained 294.95âMb of transposable elements, accounting for 46.47% of the assembly (TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab5">5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab6">6</a>). The gene prediction was performed on a combination of <i>de novo</i>, homologous, and RNA-Seq-based approaches. It resulted in 42,609 protein-coding genes and 3,579 non-protein-coding genes, respectively (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab7">7</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0130-6/MediaObjects/41597_2019_130_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0130-6/MediaObjects/41597_2019_130_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="797" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>General description of the assembly workflow. The pipeline included removal of low quality and adapter-contaminated reads, <i>de novo</i> assembly, construction of linkage map, chromosome-scale assembly, and genome annotation.</p></div></div><div xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Materials</h3><p>All plant accessions were provided by Hubei Natural Science Resource Center for Edible Legumes in Wuhan of China. A single plant of the widely cultivated asparagus bean variety âXiabao IIâ (<i>Vigna unguiculata</i> ssp. <i>sesquipedialis</i> var. âXiabao IIâ) was used for <i>de novo</i> sequencing and genome assembly. A F2 sequencing population was obtained for making the genetic map according to the following procedure. First, the F1 population were obtained by crossing âXiabao IIâ (male, same plant used for <i>de novo sequencing</i>) with a cultivar from the other subspecies, âDuanjiangdouâ (<i>Vigna unguiculata</i> ssp. <i>unguiculata</i> var. âDuanjiangdouâ; female). This step yielded 17 seeds, from which only 12 seeds survived till flowering. These F1 individuals were bagged to promote selfing, which produced 561 seeds in total (the F2 generation). Only 367 of the F2 individuals were able to germinate and mature into full plants. We selected 97 of the 367 F2 individuals for genome sequencing and genetic map construction.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Whole-genome shotgun sequencing</h3><p>Young leaves were collected from a single âXiabao IIâ plant and used for genomic DNA extraction by the CTAB method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Porebski, S., Bailey, L. G. &amp; Baum, B. R. Modification of a CTAB DNA Extraction Protocol for Plants Containing High Polysaccharide and Polyphenol Components. Plant Molecular Biology Reporter, 15, 8â15 (1997)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d42154e3976">16</a></sup>. About 10âÂµg of genomic DNA were used for library construction. Four short-insert libraries (350âbp, 445âbp, 758âbp, and 912âbp) and five large-insert libraries (2âkb, 3âkb, 5âkb, 9âkb, and 15âkb) were constructed with NEBNext Ultra II DNA Kit (NEB, America) and Nextera Mate Pair Sample Preparation Kit (Illumina, America), respectively. These libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 4000 platform. To ensure high-quality reads for the subsequent <i>de novo</i> assembly step, we filtered out the low-quality data by the following criteria: (a) reads with &gt;2% unidentified nucleotides (N) or with poly-A structure; (b) reads with â¥40% bases having low quality for short insert-size libraries and â¥60% for large insert-size libraries; (c) reads with adapters or PCR duplication; (d) reads with 20âbp in 5â² terminal and 5âbp in 3â² terminal. Subsequently, about 222.9âGb clean data were retrieved<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="&#xA;                           China National GeneBank, https://db.cngb.org/search/?q=CNP0000264&amp;from=CNSA (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d42154e3983">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="&#xA;                           NCBI Sequence Read Archive, &#xA;                    https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc.sra:SRP144706&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d42154e3986">18</a></sup>, covering 341.66-fold of the estimated genome (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab1">1</a>).</p><p>The genomic DNA was extracted with the same procedure for the parents and all 97 F2 individuals in the resequencing population. Each DNA was used to construct 500âbp insert size libraries, which were then sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 4000 platform. Each individual was sequenced to at least 4Ã coverage. NGSQCToolkit_v2.3.3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Patel, R. K. &amp; Jain, M. NGS QC Toolkit: A Toolkit for Quality Control of Next Generation Sequencing Data. PLoS One 7(2), e30619 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d42154e3996">19</a></sup> was used to filter low-quality reads (parameters: âl 70 âs 25) and trim the poor-quality terminal bases (parameters: âl 5 âr 5). A total of 882.67âGb clean bases were kept, which represented 99% of the raw sequencing data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="&#xA;                           China National GeneBank, https://db.cngb.org/search/?q=CNP0000264&amp;from=CNSA (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4000">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="&#xA;                           NCBI Sequence Read Archive, &#xA;                    https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc.sra:SRP144706&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4003">18</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Estimation of the genome size</h3><p>The genome size of asparagus bean was estimated by the <i>k</i>-mer analysis approach using 69.42âGb filtered short-insert sequencing data. The number of effective <i>k</i>-mers and the peak depth of a series of <i>k</i> values (17, 19, 21, 23, 25, 27 29 and 31) were generated by Jeffyfish (v2.2.6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Marcais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764â770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4024">20</a></sup> with the C-setting and the genome size was estimated to be about 590âMb, according to the formula Genome_Sizeâ=â(Total <i>k</i>-mers - Erroneous <i>k</i>-mers)/Peak_depth (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab2">2</a>). It is worth noting that this number could be an underestimate, in that the GC rich regions and repetitive sequences could not be properly resolved by <i>k-</i>mer analysis. Nonetheless, our estimated genome size was within the range of previously reported sizes (560.3 Mb<sup>11</sup>~620 Mb<sup>6</sup>). The rate of genome heterozygosity was calculated with the <i>k</i>-mer frequency distribution by the GenomeScope (v1.0.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Vurture, G. W. et al. GenomeScope: fast reference-free genome profiling from short reads. Bioinformatics 33, 2202â2204 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4049">21</a></sup> and the result was around 0.77% (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Fig2">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">
                           <i>De novo</i> genome assembly</h3><p>Clean data from short insert-size libraries were corrected with the Error Correction program in SOAPdenovo package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. GigaScience 1, 1â6 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4068">15</a></sup>. Genome assembly was performed based on the <i>de Bruijn</i> graph algorithm using SOAPdenovo package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Boetzer, M. &amp; Pirovano, W. SSPACE-LongRead: scaffolding bacterial draft genomes using long read sequence information. BMC Bioinformatics 15, 211 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4075">22</a></sup> by the following steps: (1) the paired-end reads of all libraries were used to construct the contig sequences while the <i>K</i>-mer values were set as 95 and 85 at the pregraph step and map step, respectively; (2) mapped paired reads were used to construct scaffolds; (3) The GapCloser package was used to map reads to the flanking sequences of gaps and to close gaps between the scaffolds; (4) genome sequence was randomly broken to re-scaffold with SSPACE package. Gaps were then filled again by GapCloser to obtain the final assembly. In the end, there were 54,864 out of 80,696 contigs with sizes longer than 1âkb. The total length of the contig assembly was 549.81âMb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab3">3</a>). The longest scaffold was 14,145,393âbp, and a total of 5,621 scaffolds were longer than 1,000 bp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="&#xA;                           China National GeneBank, https://db.cngb.org/search/?q=CNP0000264&amp;from=CNSA (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4086">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xia, Q. J. An improved genome assembly and genetic linkage map for asparagus bean, Vigna unguiculata ssp. sesquipedialis. Figshare, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.8131823&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4089">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="&#xA;                           GenBank, &#xA;                    https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc:CP039345&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4089_1">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="&#xA;                           GenBank, &#xA;                    https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc:CP039355&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4092">25</a></sup>. The total length of the scaffold assembly was 632.8âMb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab3">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">High-density genetic map construction and genome assembly anchoring</h3><p>All clean data obtained from the two parents and the 97 F2 individuals were mapped to the asparagus bean scaffold assembly using the Burrows-Wheeler-Alignment tool (BWA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4108">26</a></sup> mem algorithm. The SAM files were converted to BAM files using SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4112">27</a></sup>. Then the bam files were used to call SNP by the GATK software package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Patel, R. K. &amp; Jain, M. NGS QC Toolkit: A Toolkit for Quality Control of Next Generation Sequencing Data. PLoS One 7(2), e30619 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4116">19</a></sup> with parameters â-T HaplotypeCaller -stand_call_conf 30.0 -stand_emit_conf 10.0â and â-T SelectVariants -selectType SNPâ. The SNPs were filtered using GATK with parameters as the following:âfilterExpression âQD &lt;2.0 || ReadPosRankSum &lt;â8.0 || FS &gt;60.0 || MQ &lt;40.0 || SOR &gt;3.0 || MQRankSum &lt;â10.0 || QUAL &lt;30â âlogging_level ERRORâmissingValuesInExpressionsShouldEvaluateAsFailing. After genotyping, the raw SNPs were filtered with the following criteria: missing rate &lt;0.3 and heterozygous genotypes &lt;0.5, resulting in a total of 836,933 high-confidence SNPs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Xia, Q. J. An improved genome assembly and genetic linkage map for asparagus bean, Vigna unguiculata ssp. sesquipedialis. Figshare, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.8131823&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4120">23</a></sup>.</p><p>For the genetic map construction, 50 SNPs were selected to generate bin markers from the two termini and middle part of each scaffold. These bin markers were grouped into 11 linkage groups by JoinMap v4.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Behrend, A., Borchert, T., Spiller, M. &amp; Hohe, A. AFLP-based genetic mapping of the âbud-floweringâ trait in heather (Calluna vulgaris). BMC Genetics 14, 64 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4127">28</a></sup> with the regression mapping algorithm. The grouped bins were then sorted and genetic distance was calculated by MSTmap with the Kosambi model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Wu, Y., Bhat, P. R., Close, T. J. &amp; Lonardi, S. Efficient and accurate construction of genetic linkage maps from the minimum spanning tree of a graph. PLoS Genet 4, e1000212 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4131">29</a></sup>. According to this linkage map, scaffolds were anchored onto 11 pseudo-chromosomes. The SNPs were then assigned chromosome positions and a sliding window method (window size of 50 SNPs; step size of one SNP) was adopted to identify recombination events for each individual. All the recombination sites were merged and sorted with 20âkb intervals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Ling, H. Q. et al. Genome sequence of the progenitor of wheat A subgenome Triticum urartu. Nature 557, 424â428 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4135">30</a></sup>. In the end, the filtered 536,824 SNPs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Xia, Q. J. An improved genome assembly and genetic linkage map for asparagus bean, Vigna unguiculata ssp. sesquipedialis. Figshare, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.8131823&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4139">23</a></sup> were combined into 2,013 bins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Xia, Q. J. An improved genome assembly and genetic linkage map for asparagus bean, Vigna unguiculata ssp. sesquipedialis. Figshare, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.8131823&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4143">23</a></sup>. According to the distribution of bins, low-recombination regions were indicated (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Fig3">3</a>). These were used to construct 11 linkage maps, resulting in 2180.14âcM spanning the whole genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Xia, Q. J. An improved genome assembly and genetic linkage map for asparagus bean, Vigna unguiculata ssp. sesquipedialis. Figshare, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.8131823&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4151">23</a></sup>, which was within the reported genetic map size ranged from 643 cM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Muchero, W., Ehlers, J. D., Close, T. J. &amp; Roberts, P. A. Mapping QTL for drought stress-induced premature senescence and maturity in cowpea [Vigna unguiculata (L.) Walp.]. Theor Appl Genet 118, 849â863 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4155">31</a></sup> to 2,670 cM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="OuÃ©draogo, J. T. et al. An improved genetic linkage map for cowpea (Vigna unguiculata L.) Combining AFLP, RFLP, RAPD, biochemical markers, and biological resistance traits. Genome 45, 175â188 (2002)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4159">32</a></sup>. It is worth noting that the map lengths could be inflated by genotyping errors, as well as some biological phenomena causing double recombinations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Cartwright, D. A., Troggio, M., Velasco, R. &amp; Gutin, A. Genetic mapping in the presence of genotyping errors. Genetics 176, 2521â2527 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4163">33</a></sup>. The sliding window method and bin markers were used to reduce genotype errors. Since the parents were not 100% homozygous, the F1 plants were not identical, which might result inflated genetic size. In addition, 1,556 scaffolds with 597.52âMb were anchored<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Xia, Q. J. An improved genome assembly and genetic linkage map for asparagus bean, Vigna unguiculata ssp. sesquipedialis. Figshare, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.8131823&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4167">23</a></sup>, accounting for 94.42% of the assembled genome (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab4">4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0130-6/MediaObjects/41597_2019_130_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0130-6/MediaObjects/41597_2019_130_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="318" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The distribution of bin markers. Black arrows indicated the low-recombination regions.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Transposable elements annotation</h3><p>Transposable elements (TEs) annotation were performed by a combination of homology-based and <i>de novo</i> prediction approaches. Homology-based approach involved searching commonly used databases for known TEs at both DNA and protein level. With default parameters, RepeatMasker 3.3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to Identify Repetitive Elements in Current Protocols in Bioinformatics Ch.4 (Wiley Interscience, 2009)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4203">34</a></sup> was used to identify TEs against the Repbase TE library 18.07<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Bao, W., Kojima, K. K. &amp; Kohany, O. Repbase Update, a database of repetitive elements in eukaryotic genomes. Mob DNA 6, 11 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4207">35</a></sup> and RepeatProteinMask<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to Identify Repetitive Elements in Current Protocols in Bioinformatics Ch.4 (Wiley Interscience, 2009)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4211">34</a></sup> was used to identify TEs at the protein level in the genome assembly. For <i>de novo</i> prediction, RepeatModeler software (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>) was used in constructing the <i>de novo</i> repeat library. Tandem repeats were then predicted by TRF<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Research 2, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4229">36</a></sup> with parameters set to âMatchâ=â2, Mismatchâ=â7, Deltaâ=â7, PMâ=â80, PIâ=â10, Minscoreâ=â50 and MaxPeriodâ=â2000â. In total, we identified 294.95âMb of the transposable elements, accounting for 46.47% of the asparagus bean genome (TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab5">5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab6">6</a>). Among all TEs, long terminal repeat (LTR), which are important determinants of angiosperm genome size variation, constituted 19.24% of the assembled genome. DNA TEs accounted for 7.2% of the total sequence.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Gene annotation</h3><p>We used <i>de novo</i>, homology and RNA-Seq-based prediction methods to annotate protein-coding genes in the asparagus bean genome. Three <i>de novo</i> prediction programs, Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Stanke, M., Steinkamp, R., Waack, S. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Research 32, W309âW312 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4253">37</a></sup>, Genscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Burge, C. &amp; Karlin, S. Prediction of Complete Gene Structures in Human Genomic DNA. J. Mol. Biol 268, 78â94 (1997)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4257">38</a></sup> and GlimmerHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Burge, C. &amp; Karlin, S. Prediction of Complete Gene Structures in Human Genomic DNA. J. Mol. Biol 268, 78â94 (1997)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4261">38</a></sup> were used to annotate protein-coding genes while gene model parameters were trained from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. For homology-based prediction, protein sequences of all the protein-coding genes of eleven species including common bean (<i>Phaseolus vulgaris</i>), soybean (<i>Glycine max</i>), pigeonpea (<i>Cajanus cajan</i>), chickpea (<i>Cicer arietinum</i>), mungbean (<i>Vigna radiata</i>), adzuki bean (<i>Vigna angularis</i>), lotus (<i>Lotus japonicus</i>), medick (<i>Medicago truncatula</i>), Arabidopsis (<i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>), grape (<i>Vitis vinifera</i>), and rice (<i>Orzya sativa</i>), were first mapped to the asparagus bean genome using TblastN with the parameter E-valueâ=â10<sup>â5</sup>. GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Birney, E. &amp; Durbin, R. Using GeneWise in the Drosophila Annotation Experiment. Genome Research 10, 547â548 (2000)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4306">39</a></sup> was then used to predict gene structure within each protein-coding region. RNA-Seq data of root and stem tissues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Xia, Q. J. RNA-Seq data of root and stem tissues of asparagus bean. Figshare, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.8131535&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4310">40</a></sup> were aligned to the asparagus bean genome using TopHat on default settings. Finally, the predicted genes were merged by EvidenceModeler (EVM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4314">41</a></sup> to generate a consensus and non-redundant gene set. This process produced 42,609 protein-coding genes with an average length of 3,156âbp (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab7">7</a>).</p><p>With BLASTP (E-valueââ¤â10<sup>â5</sup>), gene functions were assigned according to the best hit of alignment to SwissProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Research 1, 45â48 (2000)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4326">42</a></sup>, TrEMBL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Boeckmann, B. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Research 31, 365â370 (2003)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4330">43</a></sup>, and KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes. Nucleic Acids Research 1, 27â30 (2000)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4334">44</a></sup> database. Functional domains and motifs of asparagus bean genes were determined by InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236â1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4338">45</a></sup>, which analyzed peptide sequences against protein databases including SMART, ProDom, Pfam, PRINTS, PROSITE and PANTHER. Gene Ontology (GO) terms for each gene were extracted from the corresponding InterPro entries. The result showed that 75.40% (32,126) of the total genes were supported by TrEMBL, 56.22% (23,953) by Swiss-Prot, and 59.27% (25,254) by InterPro. In addition, 10,096 (23.69%) genes could not be functionally annotated with current databases (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab8">8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-9"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab8" data-test="table-caption">Table 8 Functional annotation of predicted genes in asparagus bean genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6/tables/9"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The tRNA genes were identified by tRNAscan-SE software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Schattner, P., Brooks, A. N. &amp; Lowe, T. M. The tRNAscan-SE, snoscan and snoGPS web servers for the detection of tRNAs and snoRNAs. Nucleic Acids Res 33, W686âW689 (2005)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4536">46</a></sup> with default parameters. The rRNA genes were identified based on homology search to previously published plant rRNA sequences using BLASTN with parameters of âE-valueâ=â10<sup>â5</sup>â. The snRNA and miRNA genes were identified by INFERNAL v1.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335â1337 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4542">47</a></sup> software against the Rfam database with default parameters. In all, 3,579 non-protein-coding genes were identified in the asparagus bean genome, including 1593 tRNAs, 1,076 rRNAs, 350 snRNAs, and 210 microRNAs (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab9">9</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-10"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab9" data-test="table-caption">Table 9 Annotation of non-coding RNA in asparagus bean genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6/tables/10"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Data Records"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Data Records</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>The authors declare that all data reported here are fully and freely available from the date of publication. The data resulting from each experimental and analytic step are indicated in a table (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab10">10</a>). Raw read files of genome sequencing are available at NCBI Sequence Read Archive<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="&#xA;                           NCBI Sequence Read Archive, &#xA;                    https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc.sra:SRP144706&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4967">18</a></sup> and CNSA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="&#xA;                           China National GeneBank, https://db.cngb.org/search/?q=CNP0000264&amp;from=CNSA (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d42154e4971">17</a></sup>. The SNP sets of each pseudo chromosome, the anchored scaffolds information, the filtered SNPs set identified by GATK, the information of bin markers and the linkage map constructed by bin markers are deposited in figshare<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Xia, Q. DNA concentrations were normalized to 100âng/Âµl for subsequent library construction.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Assessment of the genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>Completeness of the genome assembly was assessed with default settings using the Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: Assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d42154e5289">48</a></sup> approach with a total of 1440 orthologue groups from the Embryophyta Dataset. The results showed that 93.2% of the core orthologs could be found in the asparagus bean genome, indicating a high-integrity assembly superior to the other four legume genomes. We aligned the raw reads from short insert-size sequencing back to the assembly and showed that approximately 94.88% of short reads could be successfully mapped. Furthermore, a previously reported high-density linkage map (âZZâ linkage map v.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Xu, P. et al. Genomic regions, cellular components and gene regulatory basis underlying pod length variations in cowpea (V. unguiculata L. Walp). Plant Biotechnology Journal 15, 547â557 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d42154e5293">5</a></sup> was used to assess the quality of anchored scaffolds. The sequences of 7,964 SNPs markers were aligned onto the 11 pseudo chromosomes using BLAT with parameters of â-fineâ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Kent, W. J. BLATâthe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d42154e5297">49</a></sup>. High accordance was shown between the assembled genome and the linkage map (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Fig4">4</a>). Whole genome comparative analysis was also conducted between this assembly and the cowpea genome available from Phytozome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Lonardi, S. et al. The genome of cowpea (Vigna unguiculata [L.] Walp.). Plant J 98, 767â782 (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d42154e5304">11</a></sup> by the MUMmer with nucmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biology 5, R12.11âR12.19 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d42154e5309">50</a></sup>, presenting high collinearities with four inconsistent areas, which are located in the low-recombination regions (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Fig5">5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0130-6/MediaObjects/41597_2019_130_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0130-6/MediaObjects/41597_2019_130_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="493" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Synteny between asparagus bean pseudo-chromosomes and âZZ v.2â linkage map. Each linkage on the right corresponds to one chromosome on the left with lines.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0130-6/MediaObjects/41597_2019_130_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0130-6/MediaObjects/41597_2019_130_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="191" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Comparative genome analysis between Xiabao II and IT97K-499-35<sup>a</sup>. Black arrows indicated the inconsistent areas between these two genomes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Comparison of asparagus bean genome with published common cowpea genomes</h3><p>A comparison was performed (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#Tab11">11</a>) between the asparagus bean genome and previously published common cowpea assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Munoz-Amatriain, M. et al. Genome resources for climate-resilient cowpea, an essential crop for food security. Plant J 89, 1042â1054 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d42154e5364">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Spriggs, A. et al. Assembled genomic and tissue-specific transcriptomic data resources for two genetically distinct lines of Cowpea (Vigna unguiculata (L.) Walp). Gates Open Research 2, 1â13 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d42154e5367">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Lonardi, S. et al. The genome of cowpea (Vigna unguiculata [L.] Walp.). Plant J 98, 767â782 (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d42154e5370">11</a></sup>. The asparagus bean genome assembly (549.81âMb, non-N) was significantly larger than the first published IT97K-499-35<sup>b</sup> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Munoz-Amatriain, M. et al. Genome resources for climate-resilient cowpea, an essential crop for food security. Plant J 89, 1042â1054 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d42154e5376">8</a></sup>. Its size was close to the other three common cowpea survey assemblies (IT97K-499-35<sup>a11</sup>,IT97K-499-35<sup>c</sup> and IT86D-1010)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Spriggs, A. et al. Assembled genomic and tissue-specific transcriptomic data resources for two genetically distinct lines of Cowpea (Vigna unguiculata (L.) Walp). Gates Open Research 2, 1â13 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d42154e5385">10</a></sup>. The scaffold N50 size of our asparagus bean genome was 2.7âMb, longer than the other three genomes assembled by the next-generation sequencing technology. Moreover, the asparagus bean assembly had about 94% of the scaffolds anchored into 11 pseudo-chromosomes according to the high-density genetic map. In addition, a set of 42,287 common cowpea coding sequences (CDS) derived from the single molecule real-time technology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Lonardi, S. et al. The genome of cowpea (Vigna unguiculata [L.] Walp.). Plant J 98, 767â782 (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d42154e5389">11</a></sup> could be blasted back to our asparagus bean genome with 90% similarity. All these results showed that the asparagus bean genome was of high quality.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-12"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab11" data-test="table-caption">Table 11 Comparisons of other four published cowpea assemblies.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-019-0130-6/tables/12"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section>

              <p>All tools used in this study were properly cited in the sections above. Settings and parameters were also clearly described.</p>

                   id="title-link-3">
                  Chromosome-level genome assembly of golden pompano (Trachinotus ovatus) in the family Carangidae
                </a>
              </h3>

                    id="authors-3">
